     <p><b>Boston University</b> (most commonly referred to as <b>BU</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> research university located in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>. The university is <a href="/wiki/Nonsectarian" title="Nonsectarian">nonsectarian</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Nonsectarian1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nonsectarian1-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> but was once historically affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boston_University_-_Religious_Affiliation_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boston_University_-_Religious_Affiliation-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university has more than 4,000 faculty members and 29,000 students,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> and is one of Boston's largest employers.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> It offers <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">bachelor's degrees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degrees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_degree" title="Doctoral degree" class="mw-redirect">doctoral degrees</a>, and medical and dental degrees through 18 schools and colleges on two urban campuses. The main campus is situated along the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a> in Boston's <a href="/wiki/Fenway-Kenmore" title="Fenway-Kenmore" class="mw-redirect">Fenway-Kenmore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allston,_Massachusetts" title="Allston, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">Allston</a> neighborhoods, while the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Medical_Campus" title="Boston University Medical Campus">Boston University Medical Campus</a> is in Boston's <a href="/wiki/South_End" title="South End" class="mw-redirect">South End</a> neighborhood. BU also operates 75 <a href="/wiki/Study_abroad" title="Study abroad" class="mw-redirect">study abroad</a> programs in over 33 cities in over 20 countries and has internship opportunities in 10 different countries (including the United States and abroad).</p> 
     <p>BU is categorized as an RU/VH Research University (very high research activity) in the Carnegie Classification of Institutions of Higher Education.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_Foundation_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie_Foundation-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2009–2010, BU had research expenditures of $407.8 million, or $553 million if the research led by the Medical School faculty at Boston Medical Center is included.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> BU is a member of the Boston Consortium for Higher Education<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Association of American Universities.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Terriers" title="Boston University Terriers">Boston University Terriers</a> compete in the <a href="/wiki/NCAA" title="NCAA" class="mw-redirect">NCAA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">Division I</a>. BU athletic teams compete in the <a href="/wiki/Patriot_League" title="Patriot League">Patriot League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hockey_East" title="Hockey East">Hockey East</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Athletic_Association" title="Colonial Athletic Association">Colonial Athletic Association</a> conferences, and their mascot is Rhett the Boston Terrier. Boston University is well known for <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Terriers_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Boston University Terriers men's ice hockey">men's hockey</a>, in which it has won five national championships, most recently in 2009.</p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2>

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predecessor_institutions_and_University_Charter">Predecessor institutions and University Charter</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Predecessor institutions and University Charter">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Boston University traces its roots to the establishment of the Newbury Biblical Institute in <a href="/wiki/Newbury_(town),_Vermont" title="Newbury (town), Vermont">Newbury, Vermont</a> in 1839, and was chartered with the name &quot;Boston University&quot; by the Massachusetts Legislature in 1869. The University organized formal Centennial observances both in 1939 and 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-history_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>On April 24–25, 1839 a group of <a href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist" class="mw-redirect">Methodist</a> ministers and laymen at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Bromfield_Street_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Old Bromfield Street Church (page does not exist)">Old Bromfield Street Church</a> in Boston elected to establish a Methodist theological school. Set up in Newbury, Vermont, the school was named the Newbury Biblical Institute.</p> 
     <p>In 1847, the <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational</a> Society in <a href="/wiki/Concord,_New_Hampshire" title="Concord, New Hampshire">Concord, New Hampshire</a>, invited the Institute to relocate to Concord and made available a disused Congregational church building with a capacity of 1200 people. Other citizens of Concord covered the remodeling costs. One stipulation of the invitation was that the Institute remain in Concord for at least 20 years. The charter issued by <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> designated the school the &quot;Methodist General Biblical Institute&quot;, but it was commonly called the &quot;Concord Biblical Institute.&quot;</p> 
     <p>With the agreed twenty years coming to a close, the Trustees of the Concord Biblical Institute purchased 30 acres (120,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) on Aspinwall Hill in Brookline, Massachusetts as a possible relocation site. The Institute moved in 1867 to 23 Pinkney Street in Boston and received a Massachusetts Charter as the &quot;Boston Theological Institute.&quot;</p> 
     <p>In 1869, three Trustees of the Boston Theological Institute obtained from the Massachusetts Legislature a charter for a university by name of &quot;Boston University.&quot; These three were successful Boston businessmen and Methodist laymen, with a history of involvement in educational enterprises and became the Founders of Boston University. They were <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isaac_Rich&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Isaac Rich (page does not exist)">Isaac Rich</a> (1801–1872), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lee_Claflin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lee Claflin (page does not exist)">Lee Claflin</a> (1791–1871), and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacob_Sleeper&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jacob Sleeper (page does not exist)">Jacob Sleeper</a> (1802–1889), for whom Boston University's three <a href="/wiki/West_Campus" title="West Campus">West Campus</a> dormitories are named. Lee Claflin's son, <a href="/wiki/William_Claflin" title="William Claflin">William</a>, was then Governor of Massachusetts and signed the University Charter on May 26, 1869 after it was passed by the Legislature.</p> 
     <p>As reported by Kathleen Kilgore in her book, &quot;Transformations, A History of Boston University&quot; (see <a href="/wiki/Boston_University#Further_reading" title="Boston University">Further Reading</a>), the Founders directed the inclusion in the Charter of the following provision, unusual for its time:</p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       No instructor in said University shall ever be required by the Trustees to profess any particular religious opinions as a test of office, and no student shall be refused admission . . . on account of the religious opinions he may entertain; provided, nonetheless, that this section shall not apply to the theological department of said University.
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Every department of the new university was also open to all on an equal footing regardless of sex, race, or (with the exception of the School of Theology) religion.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_.281870.E2.80.931900.29">Early years (1870–1900)</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early years (1870–1900)">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The Boston Theological Institute was absorbed into Boston University in 1871 as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_School_of_Theology" title="Boston University School of Theology">BU School of Theology</a>.</p> 
     <p>In January 1872 Isaac Rich died, leaving the vast bulk of his estate to a trust that would go to Boston University after ten years of growth while the University was organized. Most of this bequest consisted of real estate throughout the core of the city of Boston and was appraised at more than $1.5 million. Kilgore describes this as the largest single donation to an American college or university to that time.</p> 
     <p>By December, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Boston_Fire_of_1872" title="Great Boston Fire of 1872">Great Boston Fire of 1872</a> had destroyed all but one of the buildings Rich had left to the University, and the insurance companies with which they had been insured were bankrupt. The value of his estate, when turned over to the University in 1882, was half what it had been in 1872. As a result, the University was unable to build its contemplated campus on Aspinwall Hill and the land was sold piecemeal as development sites. Street names in the area, including Claflin Road, Claflin Path, and University Road, are the only remaining evidence of University ownership in this area.</p> 
     <p>Boston University established its facilities in buildings scattered through the less fashionable parts of <a href="/wiki/Beacon_Hill,_Boston" title="Beacon Hill, Boston">Beacon Hill</a>, and later expanded into the <a href="/wiki/Boylston_Street" title="Boylston Street">Boylston Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copley_Square" title="Copley Square">Copley Square</a> area before building the Charles River Campus after 1937.</p> 
     <p>In 1876, <a href="/wiki/Borden_Parker_Bowne" title="Borden Parker Bowne">Borden Parker Bowne</a> was appointed professor of philosophy. Bowne, an important figure in the history of American religious thought, was an American Christian philosopher and theologian in the <a href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist" class="mw-redirect">Methodist</a> tradition. He is known for his contributions to <a href="/wiki/Personalism" title="Personalism">personalism</a>, a philosophical branch of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_theology" title="Liberal theology">liberal theology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The movement he led is often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Personalism#Boston_Personalism" title="Personalism">Boston Personalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1877, Boston University became the first American university to award a Ph.D. to a woman when <a href="/wiki/Helen_Magill_White" title="Helen Magill White">Helen Magill White</a> earned hers in Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-Boston_University_Women.27s.2C_Gender.2C_.26_Sexuality_Studies_Program_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boston_University_Women.27s.2C_Gender.2C_.26_Sexuality_Studies_Program-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_and_establishment_of_the_Charles_River_campus">20th century and establishment of the Charles River campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 20th century and establishment of the Charles River campus">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Marsh_Chapel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Marsh_Chapel.jpg/300px-Marsh_Chapel.jpg" width="300" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Marsh_Chapel.jpg/450px-Marsh_Chapel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Marsh_Chapel.jpg/600px-Marsh_Chapel.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Marsh_Chapel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Marsh Plaza and its surrounding buildings were one of the first completed parts of the Charles River Campus.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Seeking to unify a geographically scattered school and enable it to participate in the development of the city, school president Lemuel Murlin arranged that the school buy the present campus along the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a>. Between 1920 and 1928, the school bought the 15 acres (61,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) of land that had been reclaimed from the river by the Riverfront Improvement Association. Plans for a riverside quadrangle with a multistory administrative tower modeled on the &quot;Old Boston Stump&quot; in <a href="/wiki/Boston,_Lincolnshire" title="Boston, Lincolnshire">Boston</a>, England were scaled back in the late 1920s when the State Metropolitan District Commission used <a href="/wiki/Eminent_domain" title="Eminent domain">eminent domain</a> to seize riverfront land for <a href="/wiki/Storrow_Drive" title="Storrow Drive">Storrow Drive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Through a series of fundraising campaigns by Murlin, the school slowly filled in its new campus. By spring 1936, the student body included 10,384 men and women.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1951, Harold Case became the school's fifth president and under his direction the character of the campus changed significantly, as he sought to change the school into a national research university. The campus tripled in size to 45 acres (180,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>), and added 68 new buildings before Case retired in 1967. The first large dorms, Claflin, Rich and Sleeper Halls in <a href="/wiki/West_Campus" title="West Campus">West Campus</a> were built, and in 1965 construction began on 700 Commonwealth Avenue, later named <a href="/wiki/Warren_Towers" title="Warren Towers">Warren Towers</a>, designed to house 1800 students. Between 1961 and 1966, the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_School_of_Law" title="Boston University School of Law">BU Law Tower</a>, the <a href="/wiki/George_Sherman_Union" title="George Sherman Union">George Sherman Union</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mugar_Memorial_Library" title="Mugar Memorial Library">Mugar Memorial Library</a> were constructed in the <a href="/wiki/Brutalist_architecture" title="Brutalist architecture">Brutalist</a> style, a departure from the school's traditional architecture. The College of Engineering and <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_College_of_Communication" title="Boston University College of Communication">College of Communication</a> were housed in a former stable building and auto-show room, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Besides his efforts to expand the university into a rival for Greater Boston's more prestigious academic institutions, such as Harvard University and the Massachusetts Institute of Technology (both in Cambridge across the Charles River from the B.U. campus), Case involved himself in the start of the student/societal upheavals that came to characterize the 1960s. When a mini-squabble over editorial policy at <a href="/wiki/College_radio" title="College radio" class="mw-redirect">College radio</a> WBUR-FM – whose offices were under a tall radio antenna mast in front of the School of Public Relations and Communications (later College of Communications) – started growing in the spring of 1964, Case persuaded university trustees that the university should take over the widely heard radio station (now a major outlet for National Public Radio and still a B.U.-owned broadcast facility). The trustees okayed the firing of student managers and clamped down on programming and editorial policy, which had been led by the late Jim Thistle, later a major force in Boston's broadcast news milieu. The on-campus political dispute between Case's conservative administration and the suddenly active and mostly liberal student body led to other disputes over B.U. student print publications, such as the B.U. News and the Scarlet, a fraternity association newspaper.</p> 
     <p>The Presidency of John Silber also saw much expansion. In the late 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Lahey_Clinic" title="Lahey Clinic">Lahey Clinic</a> vacated its building at 605 Commonwealth Avenue and moved to <a href="/wiki/Burlington,_Massachusetts" title="Burlington, Massachusetts">Burlington, Massachusetts</a>. The vacated building was purchased by BU to house the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_School_of_Education" title="Boston University School of Education">School of Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Then in the late 90s, concerns over lack of a &quot;campusy&quot; feel and the physical divide between the east and the western portion of campus triggered another wave of development. The <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Student_Village" title="John Hancock Student Village">John Hancock Student Village</a> or StuVi was constructed with the intent of unifying the two campuses. This facility includes a new <a href="/wiki/Fitness_and_Recreation_Center" title="Fitness and Recreation Center">Fitness and Recreation Center</a> (FitRec), a <a href="/wiki/Agganis_Arena" title="Agganis Arena">large multipurpose arena</a>, and three new dormitories, one of which opened in 2000, the other two were finished by fall of 2009, including a 26-story new tallest on-campus building (left).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The 19 and 26-story towers, known as StuV-II, house another 960 students, and have made possible the guarantee of on-campus housing to the 80% of its 16,000 undergrads who opt for it, without shunting overflow into nearby hotels as was the practice in past years.<sup id="cite_ref-globearticle_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globearticle-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In addition to the John Hancock Student Village, other projects were also completed under Silber. These projects range from the construction of the Photonics Center for the study of light, to the construction of the Life Science and Engineering Building for interdisciplinary research,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> to the renovation of 928 Commonwealth Ave in order to create a permanent home for the School of Hospitality Administration (SHA), were complete under Silber.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_21st_century">The 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The 21st century">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BU_Fitrec.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/BU_Fitrec.jpg/300px-BU_Fitrec.jpg" width="300" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/BU_Fitrec.jpg/450px-BU_Fitrec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/BU_Fitrec.jpg/600px-BU_Fitrec.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BU_Fitrec.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Fitness and Recreation Center was built in 2004-2005
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Stuvi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Stuvi.JPG/300px-Stuvi.JPG" width="300" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Stuvi.JPG/450px-Stuvi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Stuvi.JPG/600px-Stuvi.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Stuvi.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The three 1960s dorms of 
        <a href="/wiki/West_Campus" title="West Campus">West Campus</a> on the right are dwarfed by the 26-story tower of 2009, one of three dorms in StuVi.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Dr. Robert Brown's presidency, which started in 2005, will seek to further the consolidation of campus infrastructure that was commenced by Case and continued by Silber. In particular, Brown has committed Boston University to investing $1.8 billion in the completion of its 10-year strategic plan, allocating new resources to inter-college opportunities for undergraduates, improving the campus’s academic and residential facilities, and recruiting new faculty for the University’s largest college. The strategy includes increasing the annual budget to 225 million dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The cornerstone of the plan, which calls for more campus-wide collaboration, is a focus on undergraduate education, starting with an effort to encourage cross-registration among schools and colleges and to encourage undergraduate students to take full advantage of both the liberal arts and the professional programs available.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Plans exist to strengthen the School Of Law, the School of Management, and the College of Fine Arts, including changes to the physical plant and the hiring of additional faculty. The School of Medicine plans physical improvements, including more affordable studet housing.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Plans also exist to improve general student housing. The campus computer system was updated in 2009, despite the then ongoing financial crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Plans to increase the size of the campus and improve its long, narrow shape include the purchase of <a href="/wiki/Air_rights" title="Air rights">air rights</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Turnpike" title="Massachusetts Turnpike">Mass Pike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boston_campuses_and_facilities">Boston campuses and facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Boston campuses and facilities">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The University's main Charles River Campus follows <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Avenue_(Boston)" title="Commonwealth Avenue (Boston)">Commonwealth Avenue</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_(MBTA)#B_Line" title="Green Line (MBTA)">Green Line</a>, beginning near <a href="/wiki/Kenmore_Square" title="Kenmore Square">Kenmore Square</a> and continuing for over a mile and a half to its end near the border of Boston's <a href="/wiki/Allston,_Massachusetts" title="Allston, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">Allston</a> neighborhood. The <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Bridge" title="Boston University Bridge">Boston University Bridge</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a> into <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge</a> represents the dividing line between Main Campus, where most schools and classroom buildings are concentrated, and <a href="/wiki/West_Campus" title="West Campus">West Campus</a>, home to several athletic facilities and playing fields, the large West Campus dorm, and the new John Hancock Student Village complex.</p> 
     <p>As a result of its continual expansion, the Charles River campus contains an array of architecturally diverse buildings. The College of Arts and Sciences, Marsh Chapel (site of the <a href="/wiki/Marsh_Chapel_Experiment" title="Marsh Chapel Experiment">Marsh Chapel Experiment</a>), and the School of Theology buildings are the university's most recognizable and were built in the late-1930s and 1940s in collegiate gothic style. A sizable amount of the campus is traditional Boston brownstone, especially at <a href="/wiki/Bay_State_Road" title="Bay State Road" class="mw-redirect">Bay State Road</a> and South Campus where BU has acquired almost every townhouse those areas offer. The buildings are primarily dormitories but many also serve as various institutes as well as department offices. From the 1960s–1980s many contemporary buildings were constructed including the Mugar Library, BU Law School and Warren Towers, all of which were built in the <a href="/wiki/Brutalist_architecture" title="Brutalist architecture">brutalist</a> style of architecture. The <a href="/wiki/Metcalf_Science_Center_for_Science_and_Engineering" title="Metcalf Science Center for Science and Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Metcalf Science Center for Science and Engineering</a>, constructed in 1983, might more accurately be described as <a href="/wiki/Structural_Expressionism" title="Structural Expressionism" class="mw-redirect">Structural Expressionism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Morse_Auditorium" title="Morse Auditorium">Morse Auditorium</a>, adjacent, stands in stark architectrual contrast, as it was constructed as a Jewish temple. The most recent additions to BU's campus are the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Photonics_Center" title="Boston University Photonics Center">Photonics Center</a>, Life Science and Engineering Building, The Student Village (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Fitness_and_Recreation_Center" title="Fitness and Recreation Center">FitRec Center</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agganis_Arena" title="Agganis Arena">Agganis Arena</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_School_of_Management" title="Boston University School of Management">School of Management</a>. All these buildings were built in brick, a few with a substantial amount of brownstone.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_housing">Student housing</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Student housing">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:WTB_BU_residence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/WTB_BU_residence.jpg/200px-WTB_BU_residence.jpg" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/WTB_BU_residence.jpg/300px-WTB_BU_residence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/WTB_BU_residence.jpg/400px-WTB_BU_residence.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption">

     <div class="thumb tright">

        <div class="magnify">

        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Warren_Towers" title="Warren Towers">Warren Towers</a> constitutes the second-largest non-military dorm in the country.
        <sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Sheltonhall.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Sheltonhall.JPG/200px-Sheltonhall.JPG" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Sheltonhall.JPG/300px-Sheltonhall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Sheltonhall.JPG/400px-Sheltonhall.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Sheltonhall.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Shelton Hall
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg/200px-Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg/300px-Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg/400px-Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Myles_Standish_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Built in 1925 as the 
        <a href="/wiki/Myles_Standish" title="Myles Standish">Myles Standish</a> Hotel, this building was converted to dorm space in 1949.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Housing_System" title="Boston University Housing System">Boston University Housing System</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Boston University's housing system is the nation's 10th largest among four-year colleges. BU was originally a commuter school, but the university now guarantees the option of on-campus housing for four years for all undergraduate students. Currently, 76% of the undergraduate population lives on campus. Boston University requires that all students living in dormitories be enrolled in a year-long meal plan with several combinations of meals and dining points which can be used as cash in on-campus facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-diningplanrequired_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diningplanrequired-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Housing at BU is an unusually diverse melange, ranging from individual 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Brownstone" title="Brownstone">brownstone</a> <a href="/wiki/Town_house" title="Town house" class="mw-redirect">town houses</a> and apartment buildings acquired by the school to large-scale high-rises built in the 60s and 2000s.</p> 
     <p>The large dormitories include the 1800-student <a href="/wiki/Warren_Towers" title="Warren Towers">Warren Towers</a>, the largest on campus, as well as West Campus and <a href="/wiki/Towers_(Boston_University)" title="Towers (Boston University)" class="mw-redirect">The Towers</a>. The smaller dormitory and apartment style housing are mainly located in two parts of campus: Bay State Road and the South Campus residential area. Bay State Road is a tree-lined street that runs parallel to Commonwealth Avenue and is home to the majority of BUs town houses, often called &quot;brownstones&quot;. South Campus is a student residential area south of Commonwealth Avenue and separated from the main campus by the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Turnpike" title="Massachusetts Turnpike">Massachusetts Turnpike</a>. Some of the larger buildings in that area have been converted into dormitories, while the rest of the South Campus buildings are apartments.</p> 
     <p>Boston University's newest residence and principal apartment-style housing area is officially called 33 Harry Agganis Way, StuVi2 unofficially, and is part of The John Hancock Student Village project. The north facing, 26 story building is apartment style while the south facing, 19 story building is in an 8-bedroom dormitory style suite. In total, the building houses 960 residents.</p> 
     <p>Aside from these main residential areas, smaller residential dormitories are scattered along Commonwealth Avenue.</p> 
     <p>Boston University also provides <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Housing_System#Specialty_housing" title="Boston University Housing System">specialty houses</a> or specialty floors to students who have particular interests.</p> 
     <p>All large dormitories have 24/7 security and require all students to swipe and show their school identification before entering.</p> 
     <p>At least one dorm, <a href="/wiki/Shelton_Hall_(Boston_University)" title="Shelton Hall (Boston University)">Shelton Hall</a>, is rumored to be haunted by the ghost of playwright <a href="/wiki/Eugene_O%27Neill" title="Eugene O'Neill">Eugene O'Neill</a>. O'Neill lived in what was originally room 401 (now 419) while the building was a residential hotel. He died in a hospital on November 27, 1953, and his ghost is rumored to haunt both the room and the floor. The fourth floor is now a specialty floor called the Writers' Corridor.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Hancock_Student_Village">John Hancock Student Village</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: John Hancock Student Village">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg/220px-Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg/330px-Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg/440px-Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Student_Village_II_at_Boston_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Student Village II with Student Village I in the background, as viewed from Nickerson Field
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Student_Village" title="John Hancock Student Village">John Hancock Student Village</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The Student Village is a large new residential and recreational complex covering 10 acres (40,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) between Buick Street and <a href="/wiki/Nickerson_Field" title="Nickerson Field">Nickerson Field</a>, ground formerly occupied by a <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Guard" title="United States National Guard" class="mw-redirect">National Guard</a> <a href="/wiki/Armory_(military)" title="Armory (military)">Armory</a>, which had been used by the University for indoor track and field and as a storage facility before its <a href="/wiki/Demolition" title="Demolition">demolition</a> and the start of construction. The Student Village was designed with the intention of fostering community and bridging the divide between the eastern and western portions of campus.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The dormitory of apartment suites at 10 Buick Street (often abbreviated to &quot;StuVi&quot; by students) opened to juniors and seniors in the fall of 2000. In 2002, <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Insurance" title="John Hancock Insurance">John Hancock Insurance</a> announced its sponsorship of the multi-million dollar project. In 2009, Student Village II, the 26 story luxury dorm opened at 33 Harry Agganis Way.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Agganis_Arena" title="Agganis Arena">Agganis Arena</a>, named after <a href="/wiki/Harry_Agganis" title="Harry Agganis">Harry Agganis</a>, was opened to concerts and <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">hockey</a> games in January 2005.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Agganis Arena is capable of housing 6,224 spectators for <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_athletics#Terrier_Hockey" title="Boston University athletics" class="mw-redirect">Terrier hockey</a> games, replacing the smaller <a href="/wiki/Walter_Brown_Arena" title="Walter Brown Arena">Walter Brown Arena</a>. It can also be used for concerts and shows. In March 2005, the final element of phase II of the Student Village complex, the <a href="/wiki/Fitness_and_Recreation_Center" title="Fitness and Recreation Center">Fitness and Recreation (FitRec) Center</a>, was opened, drawing large crowds from the student body. Construction on the rest of phase II, which included 19- and 26-story residential towers was finished in fall 2009.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_facilities">Other facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other facilities">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg/250px-Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg/375px-Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg/500px-Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Mugar_Memorial_Library_Exterior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> A 2006 view of the entrance to 
        <a href="/wiki/Mugar_Memorial_Library" title="Mugar Memorial Library">Mugar Memorial Library</a>, BU's primary library. This entrance was demolished in the fall of 2012 to make room for the law school expansion.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Mugar_Memorial_Library" title="Mugar Memorial Library">Mugar Memorial Library</a> is the central academic library for the Charles River Campus. It also houses the Howard Gotlieb Archival Research Center, formerly called the Twentieth Century Archive, where documents belonging to thousands of eminent figures in literature, journalism, diplomacy, the arts, and other fields are housed. Among them are <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>'s personal papers from 1965 onward,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> documents from distinguished alumnus <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr" title="Martin Luther King Jr" class="mw-redirect">Martin Luther King Jr</a>, and the recent addition of <a href="/wiki/Mary-Louise_Parker" title="Mary-Louise Parker">Mary-Louise Parker</a>'s personal papers.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/George_Sherman_Union" title="George Sherman Union">George Sherman Union</a> (GSU), located next to Mugar Memorial Library, provides students with an food court featuring many fast-food chains, including <a href="/wiki/Panda_Express" title="Panda Express">Panda Express</a> (which opened Fall 2006)<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jamba_Juice" title="Jamba Juice">Jamba Juice</a>. The GSU also provides lounge areas in which to study. The basement of the George Sherman Union is home to the BU Central lounge, which hosts concerts and other activities and events.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BU_Castle2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/BU_Castle2.jpg/250px-BU_Castle2.jpg" width="250" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/BU_Castle2.jpg/375px-BU_Castle2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/BU_Castle2.jpg/500px-BU_Castle2.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BU_Castle2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> &quot;The Castle&quot; located on Bay State Road.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/BU_Castle" title="BU Castle">&quot;The Castle&quot;</a> located on the West end of Bay State Road is one of the older buildings on campus. According to lore,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the castle was built by millionaire William Lindsay for his daughter Leslie Lindsey Mason as her wedding gift. However, she was killed when her ship, the <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">RMS Lusitania</a></i>, was torpedoed and sunk by German submarines on May 7, 1915. In fact the building was commissioned by William Lindsay for his own use in 1905, long before his daughter's honeymoon on the Lusitania.<sup id="cite_ref-BFH-BBay_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BFH-BBay-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1939, the University acquired the property by agreement with the city to repay all back taxes owed; these funds were raised through donations from, among others, Dr. William Chenery, a University Trustee.<sup id="cite_ref-castle_aquisition_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-castle_aquisition-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> It served as the residence of the University president until 1967, when President Christ-Janer found it too large for his needs as a residence and turned it to other uses. It is now a conference space. Underneath the Castle is the <a href="/wiki/BU_Castle#BU_Pub" title="BU Castle">BU Pub</a>, the only BU-operated drinking establishment on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/BU_Track_and_Tennis_Center" title="BU Track and Tennis Center">Track and Tennis Center</a> on 100 Ashford Rd houses a banked 6 lane indoor track.</p> 
     <p>Parts of the 2008 film <i><a href="/wiki/21_(2008_film)" title="21 (2008 film)">21</a></i> were filmed at The Castle after undisclosed legal reasons prevented <a href="/wiki/Robert_Luketic" title="Robert Luketic">Robert Luketic</a> from filming at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other areas around the Boston University campus, including BU's School of Management, Mugar Library and FitRec, also provided production locations for the film.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Boston_University_School_of_Education" title="Boston University School of Education">Boston University School of Education</a> located at 605 Commonwealth Avenue is housed in the original location of the <a href="/wiki/Lahey_Clinic" title="Lahey Clinic">Lahey Clinic</a>. It was the merger of two pre-existing buildings, which explains its half floors (3&frac12;, 4&frac12;, 5&frac12;, etc.).</p> 
     <p>The Florence and Chafetz Hillel House on Bay State Road is the <a href="/wiki/Hillel_House" title="Hillel House" class="mw-redirect">Hillel House</a> for the university. With four floors and a basement, the facility includes lounges, study rooms and a kosher dining hall, open during the academic year (including Passover) to students and walk-ins from the community. The first floor also includes the Granby St. Cafe as well as TVs and ping-pong, pool and foosball tables. The Hillel serves as a focal point for BU's large and active Jewish community. It hosts approximately 30 student groups, including social, cultural and religious groups and BU Students for Israel (BUSI), Holocaust Education and the Center for Jewish Learning and Experience. It hosts a plethora of programs and speakers as well as Friday and Saturday shabbat services and meals.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Weld House, the former office of the president of Boston University, is the former home of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Goddard_Weld" title="Charles Goddard Weld">Charles Goddard Weld</a>, a member of the wealthy <a href="/wiki/Weld_family" title="Weld family">Weld family</a> of Massachusetts. The adjoining Dunn House contains the Office of the Chancellor.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The current office of the president is located in the John &amp; Kathleen Silber Administrative Center at 1 Silber Way.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Barnes_%26_Noble_College_Booksellers" title="Barnes &amp; Noble College Booksellers">Barnes &amp; Noble College Booksellers</a> at Boston University is the university's bookstore, which is located on <a href="/wiki/Kenmore_Square" title="Kenmore Square">Kenmore Square</a>. Materials for others schools such as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Architectural_College" title="Boston Architectural College">Boston Architectural College</a> are also available.</p> 
     <p>Boston University opened the Center for Student Services at 100 Bay State Road in <a href="/wiki/Kenmore_Square" title="Kenmore Square">Kenmore Square</a> in August 2012. The facility contains the school's Educational Resource Center, Center for Career Development, a new dining hall (officially The Fresh Food Company at Marciano Commons, but referred to as &quot;Bay State&quot;, &quot;HundoBay&quot;, &quot;100BSR&quot;, and &quot;Marciano&quot; by students), the Writing Center, an Academic Advising Center, and numerous study spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_life">Cultural life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cultural life">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Located at the junction of <a href="/wiki/Fenway-Kenmore" title="Fenway-Kenmore" class="mw-redirect">Fenway-Kenmore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allston" title="Allston">Allston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brookline,_Massachusetts" title="Brookline, Massachusetts">Brookline</a>, the University has long enjoyed these neighborhoods' cultural offerings. In the Fenway-Kenmore area are the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Fine_Arts,_Boston" title="Museum of Fine Arts, Boston">Museum of Fine Arts</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isabella_Stewart_Gardner_Museum" title="Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum">Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum</a>, and Landsdowne Street.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (February 2013)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Allston has been Boston's largest <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">bohemian</a> neighborhood since the 1960s. Nicknamed &quot;Allston Rock City&quot;,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> the neighborhood is home to many artists and musicians, as well as a variety of caf&eacute;s, and many of Boston's small music halls.</p> 
     <p>Beyond the southern border of the campus in Brookline, Harvard Avenue offers independent and foreign films at <a href="/wiki/Coolidge_Corner_Theatre" title="Coolidge Corner Theatre">Coolidge Corner Theatre</a>, and author readings at the Brookline Booksmith. Other local destinations for campus intellectuals and culture lovers include <a href="/wiki/Symphony_Hall,_Boston" title="Symphony Hall, Boston">Symphony Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan_Hall" title="Jordan Hall">Jordan Hall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Public_Library,_McKim_Building" title="Boston Public Library, McKim Building">main branch of the Boston Public Library</a> in Copley Square, the art and commerce of fashionable <a href="/wiki/Newbury_Street" title="Newbury Street">Newbury Street</a>, and across the Charles River, the museums, shops, and galleries in <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Square" title="Harvard Square">Harvard Square</a> and elsewhere in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge</a>.</p> 
     <p>The combined proximity of so many cultural institutions, colleges, public spaces, and performance outlets, with the University's own College of Fine Arts, College of Communication, University Professors Program, and other on-campus sources for cultural energy, has enabled BU to cultivate a thriving creative community.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The George Sherman Student Union at 775 Commonwealth Avenue hosts concerts and performers at &quot;BU Central&quot; and Metcalf Hall. There are 12 <i><a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a></i> groups on campus,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> two notable of which are the co-ed group &quot;The BosTones&quot; and the all-male group the &quot;Dear Abbeys&quot;.</p> 
     <p>BU is home to the <a href="/wiki/Huntington_Theatre_Company" title="Huntington Theatre Company">Huntington Theatre Company</a> at the BU Theatre (264 Huntington Avenue), as well as <a href="/wiki/Boston_Playwrights%27_Theatre" title="Boston Playwrights' Theatre">Boston Playwrights' Theatre</a>.</p> 
     <p>BU hosts campus and non-campus musical performances in the <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Tsai" title="Gerald Tsai">Tsai</a> Performance Center at 685 Commonwealth Avenue, and the CFA Concert Hall at 855 Commonwealth Avenue.</p> 
     <p>Visual art works by students and by visiting artists are displayed in rotating exhibitions in the University's three galleries: the BU Art Gallery (BUAG) at the Stone Gallery, the 808 Gallery, and the Sherman Gallery, located respectively at 855, 808, and 775 Commonwealth Avenue. In addition, BU is associated with the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Photographic_Resource_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Photographic Resource Center (page does not exist)">Photographic Resource Center</a> located at 832 Commonwealth Avenue, which mounts several exhibitions yearly, as well as special events for student and professional photographers.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guest_and_visitor_policies">Guest and visitor policies</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Guest and visitor policies">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Prior to September 2007, Boston University had a rather restrictive visitor policy, which limited the ability of students from different dormitories to visit each other at night. This changed when a new policy approved by Brown took effect.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The new policy allows for students living on campus to swipe into any on-campus dormitory between the hours of 7&nbsp;am and 2&nbsp;am using their ID cards. Student residents can also sign in guests with photo identification at any time, day or night. Overnight visitors of the opposite sex are no longer required to seek a same-sex &quot;co-host&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> However during the week before final exams no guests are permitted in the halls overnight, and are expected to be out of the hall by 2&nbsp;am.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_transit">Mass transit</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mass transit">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Most of the buildings of the main campus are located on or near Commonwealth Avenue. The Kenmore Square area of campus (including the Boston University Bookstore, Shelton Hall and Myles Standish Hall) may be accessed using the <a href="/wiki/Kenmore_(MBTA_station)" title="Kenmore (MBTA station)">Kenmore</a> stop on the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Transportation_Authority" title="Massachusetts Bay Transportation Authority">MBTA</a> Green Line <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_%22B%22_Branch" title="Green Line &quot;B&quot; Branch">B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_%22C%22_Branch" title="Green Line &quot;C&quot; Branch">C</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_%22D%22_Branch" title="Green Line &quot;D&quot; Branch">D</a> trains. Most of the rest of the main campus may be accessed using the B trains of the Green Line between the <a href="/wiki/Blandford_Street_(MBTA_station)" title="Blandford Street (MBTA station)">Blandford Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babcock_Street_(MBTA_station)" title="Babcock Street (MBTA station)">Babcock Street</a> stops. The #57 Bus runs from Kenmore Square along Commonwealth Avenue, and into Allston and Brighton. The <a href="/wiki/MBTA_Commuter_Rail" title="MBTA Commuter Rail">MBTA Commuter Rail</a> <a href="/wiki/Framingham/Worcester_Line" title="Framingham/Worcester Line">Framingham/Worcester Line</a> also stops near campus at <a href="/wiki/Yawkey_(MBTA_station)" title="Yawkey (MBTA station)">Yawkey</a> station.</p> 
     <p>The Medical Campus is served by the #1 and CT1 crosstown buses which run along Massachusetts Avenue, as well as the #47 and CT3 crosstown buses which connect the Boston University Medical Center with the <a href="/wiki/Longwood_Medical_Area" title="Longwood Medical Area" class="mw-redirect">Longwood Medical Area</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Silver_Line_(MBTA)" title="Silver Line (MBTA)">Silver Line</a> Washington Street Branch runs the entire length of the Medical Campus, one block north of most parts of the campus; it connects Boston University Medical Center with <a href="/wiki/Tufts_Medical_Center_(MBTA_station)" title="Tufts Medical Center (MBTA station)">Tufts Medical Center</a> station and downtown Boston. The nearest underground subway station is the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(MBTA_Orange_Line_station)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (MBTA Orange Line station)">Massachusetts Avenue</a> station on the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Line_(MBTA)" title="Orange Line (MBTA)">Orange Line</a>, located three blocks north of the Medical Center.</p> 
     <p>The Boston University Shuttle (BUS) serves to connect the Charles River Campus, Boston University Theater, and the Medical Campus.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sustainability">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Boston University's sustainability committee is working to &quot;reduce energy consumption and decrease waste across the campus by concentrating on four crucial areas: recycling and waste management, energy efficiency, sustainable building development and operations, and communications, education, and outreach.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-A_Greener_BU:_Look.2C_Mom.2C_No_Trays.21_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Greener_BU:_Look.2C_Mom.2C_No_Trays.21-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> One of the University's early steps toward creating a greener campus was implementing tray-free dining, which saved both water and cash.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Greener_BU:_Look.2C_Mom.2C_No_Trays.21_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Greener_BU:_Look.2C_Mom.2C_No_Trays.21-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Moving forward, BU's newly hired Director of Sustainability, Dennis Carlberg, will continue to focus the University more intensely on sustainability issues.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Greener_BU:_Look.2C_Mom.2C_No_Trays.21_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Greener_BU:_Look.2C_Mom.2C_No_Trays.21-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2010, <a href="/wiki/The_Princeton_Review" title="The Princeton Review">The Princeton Review</a> rated BU as one of the greenest schools in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_campuses">Other campuses</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other campuses">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brussels_Campus">Brussels Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Brussels Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Brussels" title="Boston University Brussels">Boston University Brussels</a>, officially named the <b>Boston University Brussels Graduate Center</b>, and also known as BUB, is part of Boston University's Metropolitan College (MET), one of seventeen degree granting colleges that make up Boston University. In 1972 Boston University became the first major American university to offer graduate business management degrees in Europe with the opening of its campus in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, Belgium.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><br /> Due to its location in the capital of Europe, home to the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, the school places a strong emphasis on international business, and the student body comprises a diverse range of nationalities and cultures.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dubai_Campus">Dubai Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dubai Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The University opened a new dental school in <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a>, UAE in 2007. The new dental program admitted its first group of students in July, 2008. In addition to BU’s dental facilities, the campus will eventually contain a wellness center, private clinics, and a major teaching hospital. &quot;Dubai Healthcare City is a free medical zone within the Emirate of Dubai, has been developed as a world-class, academic medical community, which will be developed around the Harvard Medical School Dubai Center, Boston University Institute for Dental Research and Education, and a major university hospital&quot;, according to Kathi Ferland, director of admissions at GSDM.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.budubai.ae/">[2]</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="London_Campus">London Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: London Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg/250px-43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg" width="250" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg/375px-43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg/500px-43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:43_Harrington_Gardens_-_BU_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 43 Harrington Gardens, the main academic building for Boston University's London Campus
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Boston University's largest study abroad program is located in London, England. Boston University British Programmes offers a semester of study and work in London through their London Internship Program (LIP), as well as an adjunct non-internship program at Oxford University, St. Anne's College. Starting in Fall 2008, the programme at Oxford will only be a full academic year term, not just one semester as its been structured in the past. The LIP program combines a professional internship with coursework that examines a particular academic area in the context of Britain’s history, culture, and society and its role in modern Europe. Courses in each academic area are taught by selected British faculty exclusively to students enrolled in the Boston University program. Upon successful completion of a semester, students earn 16 Boston University credits. BU British Programmes are headquartered in South Kensington, London. The campus consists of the main building at 43 Harrington Gardens, as well as six flats that have been converted to house students. This program is open to Boston University students, as well as students at other American colleges, and enrolls between 650 to 850 students across Fall, Spring and Summer terms each year.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Los_Angeles_Campus">Los Angeles Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Los Angeles Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In Los Angeles, BU has an internship program for students to study and work in the heart of the film, television, advertising and public relations, and entertainment management and law industries. The program offers three tracks from which undergraduate and graduate students can choose: Advertising and Public Relations, Film and Television, and Entertainment Management. Graduated students have the opportunity to continue their education by enrolling in the Los Angeles Certificate Program, where students can choose either the Acting in Hollywood or the Writer in Hollywood track. Courses are taught by Boston University faculty and alumni who serve as mentors in and out of the classroom. Upon successful completion of a semester students will earn 16 Boston University credits. Students who successfully complete the Los Angeles Certificate Program will receive eight Boston University credits and a certificate from Boston University College of Fine Arts or College of Communication.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Paris_Campus">Paris Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Paris Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The Paris Center runs several programs, the largest of which is the Paris Internship Program dating from 1989. Students take language and culture courses with French faculty at the Paris Center, then are placed in internships with French businesses and organizations in the area. Students live with host families or in a dormitory for the extent of the semester. Boston University Paris also organizes exchange programs with the business school <a href="/wiki/Paris_Dauphine_University" title="Paris Dauphine University">Paris Dauphine University</a> and a yearlong program with the <i>Institut d'&eacute;tudes politiques de Paris</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sciences_Po" title="Sciences Po">Sciences Po</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington.2C_DC_Campus">Washington, DC Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Washington, DC Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In Washington, D.C., BU has internship, journalism and public relations programs. Students study in the University's building on <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">Massachusetts Avenue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dupont_Circle" title="Dupont Circle">Dupont Circle</a> and take advantage of the city by interning at different locations. In 2011 the University completed construction of a new, multistory residency to house students on the program featuring touch-less entry cards for security and suites with communal kitchens, right next to the Woodly Park/Zoo Metro stop.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The program allows students to act as Washington, D.C. correspondents for newspapers and television stations across the Northeast and New England while interning at major news outlets in the city, as well as at many PR internships in politics, government and public affairs.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sydney_Campus">Sydney Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sydney Campus">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In Sydney, BU has internship and management as well as film festival and travel writing programs that vary based on semester. Around 150 students live in the University's newly constructed building in <a href="/wiki/Chippendale,_New_South_Wales" title="Chippendale, New South Wales">Chippendale</a> developed by Tony Owen Partners.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The building uses &quot;fissures to provide maximum solar access to bedrooms as well as natural ventilation throughout the building.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The new building opened in the beginning of 2011 and features underground classrooms, a lecture hall, office space, library, and a roof patio.</p> 
     <p>Other internship and study abroad opportunities are available through the International Programs office.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Admissions.2Fdemographics">Admissions/demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Admissions/demographics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>In 2013, the university received a record-breaking 52,693 applications to fill only 3,800 seats in the Class of 2017. This was the highest amount of applications received by any university in North America with 36 percent of applicants offered admission to the Class of 2017. The students offered acceptance come from the top 9 percent of their high school graduating class, have an A- average, scored an SAT Composite Average of 2016, and an ACT Average of 30.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Based on currently enrolled student responses within the university student database 50.6% <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a>, 14% <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a>, 11.6% international students, 8.6% <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a>, and 3.2% <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black</a>. The international community at Boston University is 18% Chinese, 12% <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korean</a>, 11% <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a>, 6% <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwanese</a>, 6% Canadian, 4% Japanese, 3% <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkish</a>, 2% <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thai</a>, 2% <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabian</a>, and 2% <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexican</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Of the 7% of students who are international students, 26% are pursuing undergraduate degrees and 47% are pursuing graduate degrees, with the remaining 27% engaged in other educational activity.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> BU also has the second highest number of Jews of any private school (after <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">NYU</a>) in the country with between 3,000<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hillel_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hillel-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> and 4,000,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> or roughly 15%<sup id="cite_ref-hillel_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hillel-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> identifying as Jewish.</p> 
     <p>The plurality of registrants were from Massachusetts (19%), followed by New York (16%), New Jersey (9%), California (8%), Connecticut (4%), Pennsylvania (4%), and Texas (2%).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg/200px-Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg" width="200" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg/300px-Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg/400px-Boston_University_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences.jpg 2x" /></a>

     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BU_School_of_Management.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/BU_School_of_Management.JPG/200px-BU_School_of_Management.JPG" width="200" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/BU_School_of_Management.JPG/300px-BU_School_of_Management.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/BU_School_of_Management.JPG/400px-BU_School_of_Management.JPG 2x" /></a>

        </div> The Rafik B. Hariri Building houses the 
        <a href="/wiki/Boston_University_School_of_Management" title="Boston University School of Management">School of Management</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Sargent_College,_Boston_University,_Boston_MA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Sargent_College%2C_Boston_University%2C_Boston_MA.jpg/200px-Sargent_College%2C_Boston_University%2C_Boston_MA.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Sargent_College%2C_Boston_University%2C_Boston_MA.jpg/300px-Sargent_College%2C_Boston_University%2C_Boston_MA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Sargent_College%2C_Boston_University%2C_Boston_MA.jpg/400px-Sargent_College%2C_Boston_University%2C_Boston_MA.jpg 2x" /></a>

         <a href="/wiki/File:Sargent_College,_Boston_University,_Boston_MA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>

     <p>Boston University offers <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degrees" title="Bachelor's degrees" class="mw-redirect">bachelor's degrees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degrees" title="Master's degrees" class="mw-redirect">master's degrees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_degrees" title="Doctoral degrees" class="mw-redirect">doctoral degrees</a>, and medical and dental degrees through its 18 schools and colleges. Each school and college at the university has a three letter abbreviation, which is commonly used in place of their full school or college name. For example, the College of Arts and Sciences is commonly referred to as CAS, the School of Management is SMG, the School of Education is SED, etc.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges_and_schools">Colleges and schools</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Colleges and schools">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Colleges and schools at Boston University include:</p> 
     <dl>

         <ul> 
          <li>Graduate School of Arts and Sciences (GRS) - 1874</li> 
         </ul> </li> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Boston_University_College_of_Communication" title="Boston University College of Communication">College of Communication</a> (COM) - 1947</li> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Boston_University_College_of_Engineering" title="Boston University College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a> (ENG) - 1963</li> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Boston_University_College_of_General_Studies" title="Boston University College of General Studies">College of General Studies</a> (CGS) - 1952</li>

         <ul> 
          <li><a href="/wiki/Boston_University_Brussels" title="Boston University Brussels">Boston University Brussels</a> (BUB)</li>
